<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Rail transport</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Method of transportation</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Railway" and "Railroad" redirect here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Railway_(disambiguation)" title="Railway (disambiguation)">Railway (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Railroad_(disambiguation)" title="Railroad (disambiguation)">Railroad (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_railway_network.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2049" data-file-width="4294" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/World_railway_network.svg/350px-World_railway_network.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/World_railway_network.svg/525px-World_railway_network.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/World_railway_network.svg/700px-World_railway_network.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_railway_network.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of world railway network as of 2022</div></div></div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<p><b>Rail transport</b> (also known as <b>train transport</b>) is a means of <a href="/wiki/Transport" title="Transport">transport</a> that transfers passengers and goods on wheeled <a href="/wiki/Vehicle" title="Vehicle">vehicles</a> running on rails, which are located on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Track_(rail_transport)" title="Track (rail transport)">tracks</a>. In contrast to <a href="/wiki/Road_transport" title="Road transport">road transport</a>, where the vehicles run on a prepared flat surface, rail vehicles (<a href="/wiki/Rolling_stock" title="Rolling stock">rolling stock</a>) are directionally guided by the tracks on which they run. Tracks usually consist of <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> rails, installed on <a href="/wiki/Railroad_tie" title="Railroad tie">sleepers</a> (ties) set in <a href="/wiki/Track_ballast" title="Track ballast">ballast</a>, on which the rolling stock, usually fitted with metal wheels, moves. Other variations are also possible, such as "slab track", in which the rails are fastened to a concrete foundation resting on a prepared subsurface.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Berlin_Technikmuseum_Holzbahn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Berlin_Technikmuseum_Holzbahn.jpg/220px-Berlin_Technikmuseum_Holzbahn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Berlin_Technikmuseum_Holzbahn.jpg/330px-Berlin_Technikmuseum_Holzbahn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Berlin_Technikmuseum_Holzbahn.jpg/440px-Berlin_Technikmuseum_Holzbahn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Berlin_Technikmuseum_Holzbahn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>16th-century minecart, an early example of unpowered rail transport</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KTT_train.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1440" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/KTT_train.jpg/220px-KTT_train.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/KTT_train.jpg/330px-KTT_train.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/KTT_train.jpg/440px-KTT_train.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KTT_train.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>KTT set operating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guangzhou-Kowloon_through_train" title="Guangzhou-Kowloon through train">Guangdong Through Train</a> service on the <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou%E2%80%93Shenzhen_railway" title="Guangzhou–Shenzhen railway">Guangshen railway</a>, used by the <a href="/wiki/MTR_Corporation" title="MTR Corporation">MTR Corporation</a>, an example of modern rail transport</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TRA_DR2800_Tze-Chiang_Ltd-Exp_Yilan_Line_Sijiaoting_20190220.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2700" data-file-width="3600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/TRA_DR2800_Tze-Chiang_Ltd-Exp_Yilan_Line_Sijiaoting_20190220.JPG/220px-TRA_DR2800_Tze-Chiang_Ltd-Exp_Yilan_Line_Sijiaoting_20190220.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/TRA_DR2800_Tze-Chiang_Ltd-Exp_Yilan_Line_Sijiaoting_20190220.JPG/330px-TRA_DR2800_Tze-Chiang_Ltd-Exp_Yilan_Line_Sijiaoting_20190220.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/TRA_DR2800_Tze-Chiang_Ltd-Exp_Yilan_Line_Sijiaoting_20190220.JPG/440px-TRA_DR2800_Tze-Chiang_Ltd-Exp_Yilan_Line_Sijiaoting_20190220.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TRA_DR2800_Tze-Chiang_Ltd-Exp_Yilan_Line_Sijiaoting_20190220.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/DR2800_series" title="DR2800 series">DR2800 series</a> passing <a href="/wiki/Sijiaoting_railway_station" title="Sijiaoting railway station">Sijiaoting railway station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ruifang_District" title="Ruifang District">Ruifang District</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Taipei" title="New Taipei">New Taipei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SL_hitoyoshi_09sum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="912" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/SL_hitoyoshi_09sum.jpg/220px-SL_hitoyoshi_09sum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/SL_hitoyoshi_09sum.jpg/330px-SL_hitoyoshi_09sum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/SL_hitoyoshi_09sum.jpg/440px-SL_hitoyoshi_09sum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SL_hitoyoshi_09sum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/SL_Hitoyoshi" title="SL Hitoyoshi">SL Hitoyoshi</a> <a href="/wiki/Steam_locomotive" title="Steam locomotive">steam</a>-hauled <a href="/wiki/Excursion_train" title="Excursion train">excursion train</a> operating between <a href="/wiki/Kumamoto" title="Kumamoto">Kumamoto</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hitoyoshi" title="Hitoyoshi">Hitoyoshi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyushu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a></div></div></div>

<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuvola_apps_ksysv_square.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Nuvola_apps_ksysv_square.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_ksysv_square.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Nuvola_apps_ksysv_square.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_ksysv_square.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Nuvola_apps_ksysv_square.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_ksysv_square.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Transport" title="Portal:Transport">Transport portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Transport" title="Template:Transport"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Transport" title="Template talk:Transport"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Transport&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Rolling stock in a rail transport system generally encounters lower <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">frictional resistance</a> than rubber-tyred road vehicles, so passenger and freight cars (carriages and wagons) can be coupled into longer <a href="/wiki/Train" title="Train">trains</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport_operations" title="Rail transport operations">operation</a> is carried out by a <a href="/wiki/Railway_company" title="Railway company">railway company</a>, providing transport between <a href="/wiki/Train_station" title="Train station">train stations</a> or freight customer facilities. Power is provided by <a href="/wiki/Locomotive" title="Locomotive">locomotives</a> which either draw <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">electric power</a> from a <a href="/wiki/Railway_electrification_system" title="Railway electrification system">railway electrification system</a> or produce their own power, usually by <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">diesel engines</a> or, historically, <a href="/wiki/Steam_locomotive" title="Steam locomotive">steam</a> engines. Most tracks are accompanied by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Railway_signaling" title="Railway signaling">signalling system</a>. Railways are a safe land transport system when compared to other forms of transport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[a]</a></sup> Railway transport is capable of high levels of passenger and cargo utilisation and energy efficiency, but is often less flexible and more <a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">capital</a>-intensive than road transport, when lower traffic levels are considered.
</p><p>The oldest known, man/animal-hauled railways date back to the 6th century BC in <a href="/wiki/Corinth" title="Corinth">Corinth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>. Rail transport then commenced in mid 16th century in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> in the form of horse-powered <a href="/wiki/Funicular" title="Funicular">funiculars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wagonway" title="Wagonway">wagonways</a>. Modern rail transport commenced with the British development of the <a href="/wiki/Steam_locomotive" title="Steam locomotive">steam locomotive</a> in Merthyr Tydfil when <a href="/wiki/Richard_Trevithick" title="Richard Trevithick">Richard Trevithick</a> ran a steam locomotive and loaded wagons between Penydarren Ironworks and Abercynon in 1802. Thus the <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain" title="Rail transport in Great Britain">railway system in Great Britain</a> is the oldest in the world. Built by <a href="/wiki/George_Stephenson" title="George Stephenson">George Stephenson</a> and his son <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stephenson" title="Robert Stephenson">Robert</a>'s company <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stephenson_and_Company" title="Robert Stephenson and Company">Robert Stephenson and Company</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Locomotion_No._1" title="Locomotion No. 1"><i>Locomotion</i> No. 1</a> is the first steam locomotive to carry passengers on a public rail line, the <a href="/wiki/Stockton_and_Darlington_Railway" title="Stockton and Darlington Railway">Stockton and Darlington Railway</a> in 1825. George Stephenson also built the first public inter-city railway line in the world to use only the steam locomotives, the <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_and_Manchester_Railway" title="Liverpool and Manchester Railway">Liverpool and Manchester Railway</a> which <a href="/wiki/Opening_of_the_Liverpool_and_Manchester_Railway" title="Opening of the Liverpool and Manchester Railway">opened in 1830</a>. With steam engines, one could construct mainline railways, which were a key component of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. Also, railways reduced the costs of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shipping" title="Shipping">shipping</a>, and allowed for fewer lost goods, compared with water transport, which faced occasional sinking of ships. The change from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canals" title="Canals">canals</a> to railways allowed for "national markets" in which prices varied very little from city to city. The spread of the railway network and the use of railway timetables, led to the standardisation of time (railway time) in Britain based on Greenwich Mean Time. Prior to this, major towns and cities varied their local time relative to GMT. The invention and development of the railway in the United Kingdom was one of the most important technological inventions of the 19th century. The world's first underground railway, the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Railway" title="Metropolitan Railway">Metropolitan Railway</a> (part of the <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">London Underground</a>), opened in 1863.
</p><p>In the 1880s, <a href="/wiki/Railway_electrification_system" title="Railway electrification system">electrified</a> trains were introduced, leading to electrification of tramways and rapid transit systems. Starting during the 1940s, the non-electrified railways in most countries had their steam locomotives replaced by <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a>-electric locomotives, with the process being almost complete by the 2000s. During the 1960s, electrified <a href="/wiki/High-speed_rail" title="High-speed rail">high-speed railway systems</a> were introduced in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and later in some other countries. Many countries are in the process of replacing diesel locomotives with electric locomotives, mainly due to environmental concerns, a notable example being <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, which has completely electrified its network. Other forms of guided ground transport outside the traditional railway definitions, such as <a href="/wiki/Monorail" title="Monorail">monorail</a> or <a href="/wiki/Maglev" title="Maglev">maglev</a>, have been tried but have seen limited use.
</p><p>Following a decline after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> due to competition from cars and aeroplanes, rail transport has had a revival in recent decades due to road congestion and rising fuel prices, as well as governments <a href="/wiki/Rail_subsidies" title="Rail subsidies">investing in rail</a> as a means of reducing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CO2_emissions" title="CO2 emissions">CO<sub>2</sub> emissions</a> in the context of concerns about <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_rail_transport" title="History of rail transport">History of rail transport</a></div>
<p>The history of rail transport began in prehistoric times.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_systems">Ancient systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Ancient systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Evidence indicates that there was 6 to 8.5 km long <i><a href="/wiki/Diolkos" title="Diolkos">Diolkos</a></i> paved trackway, which transported boats across the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Corinth" title="Isthmus of Corinth">Isthmus of Corinth</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> from around 600 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verdelis,_mikolaos_(1950stas),_526_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Verdelis,_mikolaos_(1950stas),_526-2">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cook,_R._M._(1979),_152_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cook,_R._M._(1979),_152-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drijvers,_J.W._(1992),_75_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drijvers,_J.W._(1992),_75-4">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raepsaet,_G._&amp;_Tolley,_M._(1993),_256_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raepsaet,_G._&amp;_Tolley,_M._(1993),_256-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis,_M._J._T._(2001),_11_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis,_M._J._T._(2001),_11-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Bundling_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This claim has too many footnotes for reading to be smooth. (July 2022)">excessive citations</span></a></i>]</sup> Wheeled vehicles pulled by men and animals ran in grooves in <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>, which provided the track element, preventing the wagons from leaving the intended route. The Diolkos was in use for over 650 years, until at least the 1st century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis,_M._J._T._(2001),_11_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis,_M._J._T._(2001),_11-6">[5]</a></sup> Paved trackways were also later built in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Roman Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>In China, a railway has been discovered in southwest Henan province near <a href="/wiki/Nanyang,_Henan" title="Nanyang, Henan">Nanyang</a>.  It was carbon-dated to be about 2200 years old, from the Qin dynasty. The rails were made from hard wood and treated against corrosion, while the railway ties were made from wood that was not treated and has since rotted. The Qin railway was designed to allow horses to gallop through to the next rail station, where they would be swapped with a fresh horse.  The railway is theorized to have been used for transportation of goods to the front-line troops and to fix the Great Wall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (May 2021)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-steam_modern_systems">Pre-steam modern systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Pre-steam modern systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Funicular" title="Funicular">Funicular</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wagonway" title="Wagonway">Wagonway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tramway_(industrial)" title="Tramway (industrial)">Tramway (industrial)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plateway" title="Plateway">Plateway</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wooden_rails_introduced">Wooden rails introduced</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Wooden rails introduced">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reiszug,_Hohensalzburg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Reiszug%2C_Hohensalzburg.jpg/170px-Reiszug%2C_Hohensalzburg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Reiszug%2C_Hohensalzburg.jpg/255px-Reiszug%2C_Hohensalzburg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Reiszug%2C_Hohensalzburg.jpg/340px-Reiszug%2C_Hohensalzburg.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reiszug,_Hohensalzburg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Reisszug" title="Reisszug">Reisszug</a> in 2011</div></div></div>
<p>In 1515, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matth%C3%A4us_Lang" title="Matthäus Lang">Cardinal Matthäus Lang</a> wrote a description of the <a href="/wiki/Reisszug" title="Reisszug">Reisszug</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Funicular" title="Funicular">funicular</a> railway at the <a href="/wiki/Hohensalzburg_Fortress" title="Hohensalzburg Fortress">Hohensalzburg Fortress</a> in Austria. The line originally used wooden rails and a <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a> haulage rope and was operated by human or animal power, through a <a href="/wiki/Treadwheel" title="Treadwheel">treadwheel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fm1_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-fm1-9">[8]</a></sup> The line still exists and is operational, although in updated form and is possibly the oldest operational railway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mining_cart.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2440" data-file-width="2909" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Mining_cart.jpg/220px-Mining_cart.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Mining_cart.jpg/330px-Mining_cart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Mining_cart.jpg/440px-Mining_cart.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mining_cart.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Minecart" title="Minecart">Minecart</a> shown in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Re_Metallica" title="De Re Metallica">De Re Metallica</a></i> (1556). The guide pin fits in a groove between two wooden planks.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wagonway" title="Wagonway">Wagonways</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Tramway_(industrial)" title="Tramway (industrial)">tramways</a>) using wooden rails, hauled by horses, started appearing in the 1550s to facilitate the transport of ore tubs to and from mines, and soon became popular in Europe. Such an operation was illustrated in Germany in 1556 by <a href="/wiki/Georgius_Agricola" title="Georgius Agricola">Georgius Agricola</a> in his work <a href="/wiki/De_re_metallica" title="De re metallica">De re metallica</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> This line used "Hund" carts with unflanged wheels running on wooden planks and a vertical pin on the truck fitting into the gap between the planks to keep it going the right way. The miners called the wagons <i>Hunde</i> ("dogs") from the noise they made on the tracks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are many references to their use in central Europe in the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> Such a transport system was later used by German miners at <a href="/wiki/Caldbeck" title="Caldbeck">Caldbeck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a>, England, perhaps from the 1560s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> A wagonway was built at <a href="/wiki/Prescot" title="Prescot">Prescot</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a>, sometime around 1600, possibly as early as 1594. Owned by Philip Layton, the line carried coal from a pit near Prescot Hall to a terminus about half a mile (800 m) away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> A funicular railway was also made at <a href="/wiki/Broseley" title="Broseley">Broseley</a> in <a href="/wiki/Shropshire" title="Shropshire">Shropshire</a> some time before 1604. This carried coal for James Clifford from his mines down to the <a href="/wiki/River_Severn" title="River Severn">river Severn</a> to be loaded onto barges and carried to riverside towns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wollaton_Wagonway" title="Wollaton Wagonway">Wollaton Wagonway</a>, completed in 1604 by <a href="/wiki/Huntingdon_Beaumont" title="Huntingdon Beaumont">Huntingdon Beaumont</a>, has sometimes erroneously been cited as the earliest British railway. It ran from <a href="/wiki/Strelley,_Nottingham" title="Strelley, Nottingham">Strelley</a> to <a href="/wiki/Wollaton" title="Wollaton">Wollaton</a> near <a href="/wiki/Nottingham" title="Nottingham">Nottingham</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Middleton_Railway" title="Middleton Railway">Middleton Railway</a> in <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a>, which was built in 1758, later became the world's oldest operational railway (other than funiculars), albeit now in an upgraded form. In 1764, the first railway in the Americas was built in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewiston,_New_York" title="Lewiston, New York">Lewiston, New York</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porter_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal_rails_introduced">Metal rails introduced</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Metal rails introduced">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the late 1760s, the <a href="/wiki/Coalbrookdale" title="Coalbrookdale">Coalbrookdale</a> Company began to fix plates of <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a> to the upper surface of the wooden rails. This allowed a variation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rail_gauge" title="Rail gauge">gauge</a> to be used. At first only <a href="/wiki/Balloon_loop" title="Balloon loop">balloon loops</a> could be used for turning, but later, movable points were taken into use that allowed for switching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Little_Eaton_Tramway_Replica_Wagon_small.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="966" data-file-width="1288" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Little_Eaton_Tramway_Replica_Wagon_small.jpg/220px-Little_Eaton_Tramway_Replica_Wagon_small.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Little_Eaton_Tramway_Replica_Wagon_small.jpg/330px-Little_Eaton_Tramway_Replica_Wagon_small.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Little_Eaton_Tramway_Replica_Wagon_small.jpg/440px-Little_Eaton_Tramway_Replica_Wagon_small.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Little_Eaton_Tramway_Replica_Wagon_small.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A replica of a "Little Eaton Tramway" wagon, the tracks are plateways</div></div></div>
<p>A system was introduced in which unflanged wheels ran on L-shaped metal plates –  these became known as <a href="/wiki/Plateway" title="Plateway">plateways</a>. <a href="/wiki/John_Curr" title="John Curr">John Curr</a>, a Sheffield colliery manager, invented this flanged rail in 1787, though the exact date of this is disputed. The plate rail was taken up by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Outram" title="Benjamin Outram">Benjamin Outram</a> for wagonways serving his canals, manufacturing them at his <a href="/wiki/Butterley_Company" title="Butterley Company">Butterley ironworks</a>. In 1803, <a href="/wiki/William_Jessop" title="William Jessop">William Jessop</a> opened the <a href="/wiki/Surrey_Iron_Railway" title="Surrey Iron Railway">Surrey Iron Railway</a>, a double track plateway, erroneously sometimes cited as world's first public railway, in south London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cromford_and_High_Peak_Railway_cast-iron_fishbelly_rail.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="456" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Cromford_and_High_Peak_Railway_cast-iron_fishbelly_rail.png/220px-Cromford_and_High_Peak_Railway_cast-iron_fishbelly_rail.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Cromford_and_High_Peak_Railway_cast-iron_fishbelly_rail.png/330px-Cromford_and_High_Peak_Railway_cast-iron_fishbelly_rail.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Cromford_and_High_Peak_Railway_cast-iron_fishbelly_rail.png/440px-Cromford_and_High_Peak_Railway_cast-iron_fishbelly_rail.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cromford_and_High_Peak_Railway_cast-iron_fishbelly_rail.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cast iron fishbelly edge rail manufactured by Outram at the <a href="/wiki/Butterley_Company" title="Butterley Company">Butterley Company</a> ironworks for the <a href="/wiki/Cromford_and_High_Peak_Railway" title="Cromford and High Peak Railway">Cromford and High Peak Railway</a> (1831). These are smooth edgerails for wheels with flanges.</div></div></div>
<p>Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/William_Jessop" title="William Jessop">William Jessop</a> had earlier used a form of all-iron <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edge_rail" title="Edge rail">edge rail</a> and flanged wheels successfully for an extension to the <a href="/wiki/Charnwood_Forest_Canal" title="Charnwood Forest Canal">Charnwood Forest Canal</a> at <a href="/wiki/Nanpantan" title="Nanpantan">Nanpantan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Loughborough" title="Loughborough">Loughborough</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leicestershire" title="Leicestershire">Leicestershire</a> in 1789. In 1790, Jessop and his partner Outram began to manufacture edge-rails. Jessop became a partner in the Butterley Company in 1790. The first public edgeway (thus also first public railway) built was <a href="/wiki/Lake_Lock_Rail_Road" title="Lake Lock Rail Road">Lake Lock Rail Road</a> in 1796. Although the primary purpose of the line was to carry coal, it also carried passengers.
</p><p>These two systems of constructing iron railways, the "L" plate-rail and the smooth edge-rail, continued to exist side by side until well into the early 19th century. The flanged wheel and edge-rail eventually proved its superiority and became the standard for railways.
</p><p>Cast iron used in rails proved unsatisfactory because it was brittle and broke under heavy loads. The <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">wrought iron</a> invented by <a href="/wiki/John_Birkinshaw" title="John Birkinshaw">John Birkinshaw</a> in 1820 replaced cast iron. <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">Wrought iron</a> (usually simply referred to as "iron") was a ductile material that could undergo considerable deformation before breaking, making it more suitable for iron rails. But iron was expensive to produce until <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cort" title="Henry Cort">Henry Cort</a> patented the <a href="/wiki/Puddling_(metallurgy)" title="Puddling (metallurgy)">puddling process</a> in 1784. In 1783 Cort also patented the <a href="/wiki/Rolling_(metalworking)" title="Rolling (metalworking)">rolling process</a>, which was 15 times faster at consolidating and shaping iron than hammering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> These processes greatly lowered the cost of producing iron and rails. The next important development in iron production was <a href="/wiki/Hot_blast" title="Hot blast">hot blast</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/James_Beaumont_Neilson" title="James Beaumont Neilson">James Beaumont Neilson</a> (patented 1828), which considerably reduced the amount of <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke (fuel)</a> or charcoal needed to produce pig iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> Wrought iron was a soft material that contained slag or <i>dross</i>. The softness and dross tended to make iron rails distort and delaminate and they lasted less than 10 years. Sometimes they lasted as little as one year under high traffic. All these developments in the production of iron eventually led to replacement of composite wood/iron rails with superior all-iron rails.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Railroad-_Central_of_Georgia_roundhouse,_circa_1875_-_DPLA_-_86835aba2db7ca09d2cc2e7d522eecd7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="444" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Railroad-_Central_of_Georgia_roundhouse%2C_circa_1875_-_DPLA_-_86835aba2db7ca09d2cc2e7d522eecd7.jpg/220px-Railroad-_Central_of_Georgia_roundhouse%2C_circa_1875_-_DPLA_-_86835aba2db7ca09d2cc2e7d522eecd7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Railroad-_Central_of_Georgia_roundhouse%2C_circa_1875_-_DPLA_-_86835aba2db7ca09d2cc2e7d522eecd7.jpg/330px-Railroad-_Central_of_Georgia_roundhouse%2C_circa_1875_-_DPLA_-_86835aba2db7ca09d2cc2e7d522eecd7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Railroad-_Central_of_Georgia_roundhouse%2C_circa_1875_-_DPLA_-_86835aba2db7ca09d2cc2e7d522eecd7.jpg/440px-Railroad-_Central_of_Georgia_roundhouse%2C_circa_1875_-_DPLA_-_86835aba2db7ca09d2cc2e7d522eecd7.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Railroad-_Central_of_Georgia_roundhouse,_circa_1875_-_DPLA_-_86835aba2db7ca09d2cc2e7d522eecd7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Railroad at Central of Georgia roundhouse, circa 1875.</div></div></div>
<p>The introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Bessemer_process" title="Bessemer process">Bessemer process</a>, enabling steel to be made inexpensively, led to the era of great expansion of railways that began in the late 1860s. Steel rails lasted several times longer than iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wells1891_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wells1891-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grubler1990_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grubler1990-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> Steel rails made heavier locomotives possible, allowing for longer trains and improving the productivity of railroads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> The Bessemer process introduced nitrogen into the steel, which caused the steel to become brittle with age. The <a href="/wiki/Open_hearth_furnace" title="Open hearth furnace">open hearth furnace</a> began to replace the Bessemer process near the end of the 19th century, improving the quality of steel and further reducing costs. Thus steel completely replaced the use of iron in rails, becoming standard for all railways.
</p><p>The first passenger <a href="/wiki/Horsecar" title="Horsecar">horsecar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tram" title="Tram">tram</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swansea_and_Mumbles_Railway" title="Swansea and Mumbles Railway">Swansea and Mumbles Railway</a> was opened between <a href="/wiki/Swansea" title="Swansea">Swansea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mumbles" title="Mumbles">Mumbles</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> in 1807.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> Horses remained the preferable mode for tram transport even after the arrival of steam engines until the end of the 19th century, because they were cleaner compared to steam-driven trams which caused smoke in city streets.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steam_power_introduced">Steam power introduced</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Steam power introduced">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Steam_locomotive" title="Steam locomotive">Steam locomotive</a></div>
<p>In 1784 <a href="/wiki/James_Watt" title="James Watt">James Watt</a>, a Scottish inventor and mechanical engineer, patented a design for a <a href="/wiki/Steam_locomotive" title="Steam locomotive">steam locomotive</a>. Watt had improved the <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engine</a> of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Newcomen" title="Thomas Newcomen">Thomas Newcomen</a>, hitherto used to pump water out of mines, and developed a <a href="/wiki/Reciprocating_engine" title="Reciprocating engine">reciprocating engine</a> in 1769 capable of powering a wheel. This was a large <a href="/wiki/Stationary_engine" title="Stationary engine">stationary engine</a>, powering cotton mills and a variety of machinery; the state of boiler technology necessitated the use of low-pressure steam acting upon a vacuum in the cylinder, which required a separate <a href="/wiki/Condenser_(heat_transfer)" title="Condenser (heat transfer)">condenser</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Air_pump" title="Air pump">air pump</a>. Nevertheless, as the construction of boilers improved, Watt investigated the use of high-pressure steam acting directly upon a piston, raising the possibility of a smaller engine that might be used to power a vehicle. Following his patent, Watt's employee <a href="/wiki/William_Murdoch" title="William Murdoch">William Murdoch</a> produced a working model of a self-propelled steam carriage in that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TrevithicksEngine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="786" data-file-width="1179" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/TrevithicksEngine.jpg/220px-TrevithicksEngine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/TrevithicksEngine.jpg/330px-TrevithicksEngine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/TrevithicksEngine.jpg/440px-TrevithicksEngine.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TrevithicksEngine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A replica of Trevithick's engine at the <a href="/wiki/National_Waterfront_Museum" title="National Waterfront Museum">National Waterfront Museum</a>, Swansea</div></div></div>
<p>The first full-scale working railway <a href="/wiki/Steam_locomotive" title="Steam locomotive">steam locomotive</a> was built in the United Kingdom in 1804 by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Trevithick" title="Richard Trevithick">Richard Trevithick</a>, a British engineer born in <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>. This used high-pressure steam to drive the engine by one power stroke. The transmission system employed a large <a href="/wiki/Flywheel" title="Flywheel">flywheel</a> to even out the action of the piston rod. On 21 February 1804, the world's first steam-powered railway journey took place when Trevithick's unnamed steam locomotive hauled a train along the tramway of the <a href="/wiki/Penydarren" title="Penydarren">Penydarren</a> ironworks, near <a href="/wiki/Merthyr_Tydfil" title="Merthyr Tydfil">Merthyr Tydfil</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Wales" title="South Wales">South Wales</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> Trevithick later demonstrated a locomotive operating upon a piece of circular rail track in <a href="/wiki/Bloomsbury" title="Bloomsbury">Bloomsbury</a>, London, the <i><a href="/wiki/Catch_Me_Who_Can" title="Catch Me Who Can">Catch Me Who Can</a></i>, but never got beyond the experimental stage with railway locomotives, not least because his engines were too heavy for the cast-iron plateway track then in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blenkinsop%27s_rack_locomotive,_1812_(British_Railway_Locomotives_1803-1853).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="998" data-file-width="1157" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Blenkinsop%27s_rack_locomotive%2C_1812_%28British_Railway_Locomotives_1803-1853%29.jpg/220px-Blenkinsop%27s_rack_locomotive%2C_1812_%28British_Railway_Locomotives_1803-1853%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Blenkinsop%27s_rack_locomotive%2C_1812_%28British_Railway_Locomotives_1803-1853%29.jpg/330px-Blenkinsop%27s_rack_locomotive%2C_1812_%28British_Railway_Locomotives_1803-1853%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Blenkinsop%27s_rack_locomotive%2C_1812_%28British_Railway_Locomotives_1803-1853%29.jpg/440px-Blenkinsop%27s_rack_locomotive%2C_1812_%28British_Railway_Locomotives_1803-1853%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blenkinsop%27s_rack_locomotive,_1812_(British_Railway_Locomotives_1803-1853).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>Salamanca</i> locomotive</div></div></div>
<p>The first commercially successful steam locomotive was <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Murray" title="Matthew Murray">Matthew Murray</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Rack_railway" title="Rack railway">rack</a> locomotive <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Salamanca" title="The Salamanca">Salamanca</a></i> built for the <a href="/wiki/Middleton_Railway" title="Middleton Railway">Middleton Railway</a> in <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a> in 1812. This twin-cylinder locomotive was light enough to not break the <a href="/wiki/Wagonway" title="Wagonway">edge-rails</a> track and solved the problem of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rail_adhesion" title="Rail adhesion">adhesion</a> by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cog-wheel" title="Cog-wheel">cog-wheel</a> using teeth cast on the side of one of the rails. Thus it was also the first <a href="/wiki/Rack_railway" title="Rack railway">rack railway</a>.
</p><p>This was followed in 1813 by the locomotive <i><a href="/wiki/Puffing_Billy_(locomotive)" title="Puffing Billy (locomotive)">Puffing Billy</a></i> built by <a href="/wiki/Blackett_of_Wylam" title="Blackett of Wylam">Christopher Blackett</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Hedley" title="William Hedley">William Hedley</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Wylam" title="Wylam">Wylam</a> Colliery Railway, the first successful locomotive running by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rail_adhesion" title="Rail adhesion">adhesion</a> only. This was accomplished by the distribution of weight between a number of wheels. <i>Puffing Billy</i> is now on display in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_Museum_(London)" title="Science Museum (London)">Science Museum</a> in London, and is the oldest locomotive in existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Locomotion_No._1..jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1527" data-file-width="2058" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Locomotion_No._1..jpg/220px-Locomotion_No._1..jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Locomotion_No._1..jpg/330px-Locomotion_No._1..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Locomotion_No._1..jpg/440px-Locomotion_No._1..jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Locomotion_No._1..jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>Locomotion</i> at Darlington Railway Centre and Museum</div></div></div>
<p>In 1814 <a href="/wiki/George_Stephenson" title="George Stephenson">George Stephenson</a>, inspired by the early locomotives of Trevithick, Murray and Hedley, persuaded the manager of the <a href="/wiki/Killingworth" title="Killingworth">Killingworth</a> <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">colliery</a> where he worked to allow him to build a <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam-powered</a> machine. Stephenson played a pivotal role in the development and widespread adoption of the steam locomotive. His designs considerably improved on the work of the earlier pioneers. He built the locomotive <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bl%C3%BCcher_(locomotive)" title="Blücher (locomotive)">Blücher</a></i>, also a successful <a href="/wiki/Flange" title="Flange">flanged</a>-wheel adhesion locomotive. In 1825 he built the locomotive <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Locomotion_No_1" title="Locomotion No 1">Locomotion</a></i> for the <a href="/wiki/Stockton_and_Darlington_Railway" title="Stockton and Darlington Railway">Stockton and Darlington Railway</a> in the north east of England, which became the first public steam railway in the world in 1825, although it used both horse power and steam power on different runs. In 1829, he built the locomotive <i><a href="/wiki/Stephenson%27s_Rocket" title="Stephenson's Rocket">Rocket</a></i>, which entered in and won the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainhill_Trials" title="Rainhill Trials">Rainhill Trials</a>. This success led to Stephenson establishing his company as the pre-eminent builder of steam locomotives for railways in Great Britain and Ireland, the United States, and much of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 24–30">: 24–30 </span></sup> The first public railway which used only steam locomotives, all the time, was <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_and_Manchester_Railway" title="Liverpool and Manchester Railway">Liverpool and Manchester Railway</a>, built in 1830.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Steam power continued to be the dominant power system in railways around the world for more than a century.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_power_introduced">Electric power introduced</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Electric power introduced">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Electric_locomotive" title="Electric locomotive">Electric locomotive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Railway_electrification_system" title="Railway electrification system">Railway electrification system</a></div>
<p>The first known electric locomotive was built in 1837 by chemist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Davidson_(inventor)" title="Robert Davidson (inventor)">Robert Davidson</a> of <a href="/wiki/Aberdeen" title="Aberdeen">Aberdeen</a> in Scotland, and it was powered by <a href="/wiki/Galvanic_cell" title="Galvanic cell">galvanic cells</a> (batteries). Thus it was also the earliest battery electric locomotive. Davidson later built a larger locomotive named <i>Galvani</i>, exhibited at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Scottish_Society_of_Arts" title="Royal Scottish Society of Arts">Royal Scottish Society of Arts</a> Exhibition in 1841. The seven-ton vehicle had two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct-drive" title="Direct-drive">direct-drive</a> <a href="/wiki/Reluctance_motor" title="Reluctance motor">reluctance motors</a>, with fixed electromagnets acting on iron bars attached to a wooden cylinder on each axle, and simple <a href="/wiki/Commutator_(electric)" title="Commutator (electric)">commutators</a>. It hauled a load of six tons at four miles per hour (6 kilometers per hour) for a distance of one and a half miles (2.4 kilometres). It was tested on the <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_and_Glasgow_Railway" title="Edinburgh and Glasgow Railway">Edinburgh and Glasgow Railway</a> in September of the following year, but the limited power from batteries prevented its general use. It was destroyed by railway workers, who saw it as a threat to their job security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-38">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="682" data-file-width="1135" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg/220px-First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg/330px-First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg/440px-First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lichterfelde tram, 1882</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Junarata_linnunlaulussa_1890-luvulla,_Helsinki.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2553" data-file-width="2990" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Junarata_linnunlaulussa_1890-luvulla%2C_Helsinki.jpg/220px-Junarata_linnunlaulussa_1890-luvulla%2C_Helsinki.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Junarata_linnunlaulussa_1890-luvulla%2C_Helsinki.jpg/330px-Junarata_linnunlaulussa_1890-luvulla%2C_Helsinki.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Junarata_linnunlaulussa_1890-luvulla%2C_Helsinki.jpg/440px-Junarata_linnunlaulussa_1890-luvulla%2C_Helsinki.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Junarata_linnunlaulussa_1890-luvulla,_Helsinki.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Railway in the 1890s in <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Werner_von_Siemens" title="Werner von Siemens">Werner von Siemens</a> demonstrated an electric railway in 1879 in Berlin. The world's first electric tram line, <a href="/wiki/Gross-Lichterfelde_Tramway" title="Gross-Lichterfelde Tramway">Gross-Lichterfelde Tramway</a>, opened in <a href="/wiki/Lichterfelde_(Berlin)" title="Lichterfelde (Berlin)">Lichterfelde</a> near <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>, Germany, in 1881. It was built by Siemens. The tram ran on 180 volts DC, which was supplied by running rails. In 1891 the track was equipped with an <a href="/wiki/Overhead_line" title="Overhead line">overhead wire</a> and the line was extended to <a href="/wiki/Berlin-Lichterfelde_West_station" title="Berlin-Lichterfelde West station">Berlin-Lichterfelde West station</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Volk%27s_Electric_Railway" title="Volk's Electric Railway">Volk's Electric Railway</a> opened in 1883 in <a href="/wiki/Brighton" title="Brighton">Brighton</a>, England. The railway is still operational, thus making it the oldest operational electric railway in the world. Also in 1883, <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6dling_and_Hinterbr%C3%BChl_Tram" title="Mödling and Hinterbrühl Tram">Mödling and Hinterbrühl Tram</a> opened near Vienna in Austria. It was the first tram line in the world in regular service powered from an overhead line. Five years later, in the U.S. electric <a href="/wiki/Tram" title="Tram">trolleys</a> were pioneered in 1888 on the <a href="/wiki/Richmond_Union_Passenger_Railway" title="Richmond Union Passenger Railway">Richmond Union Passenger Railway</a>, using equipment designed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frank_Julian_Sprague" title="Frank Julian Sprague">Frank J. Sprague</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:B-and-O_electric.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="485" data-file-width="729" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/B-and-O_electric.jpg/220px-B-and-O_electric.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/B-and-O_electric.jpg/330px-B-and-O_electric.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/B-and-O_electric.jpg/440px-B-and-O_electric.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:B-and-O_electric.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Baltimore &amp; Ohio electric engine</div></div></div>
<p>The first use of electrification on a main line was on a four-mile section of the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Belt_Line" title="Baltimore Belt Line">Baltimore Belt Line</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_and_Ohio_Railroad" title="Baltimore and Ohio Railroad">Baltimore and Ohio Railroad</a> (B&amp;O) in 1895 connecting the main portion of the B&amp;O to the new line to <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a> through a series of tunnels around the edges of Baltimore's downtown. Electricity quickly became the power supply of choice for subways, abetted by the Sprague's invention of multiple-unit train control in 1897. By the early 1900s most street railways were electrified.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baker_Street_Waterloo_Railway_platform_March_1906.png"><img alt='Sketch showing about a dozen people standing on an underground railway platform with a train standing at the platform. Several more people are visible inside the train, which has the words "Baker St" visible on its side.' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="654" data-file-width="907" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Baker_Street_Waterloo_Railway_platform_March_1906.png/220px-Baker_Street_Waterloo_Railway_platform_March_1906.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Baker_Street_Waterloo_Railway_platform_March_1906.png/330px-Baker_Street_Waterloo_Railway_platform_March_1906.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Baker_Street_Waterloo_Railway_platform_March_1906.png/440px-Baker_Street_Waterloo_Railway_platform_March_1906.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baker_Street_Waterloo_Railway_platform_March_1906.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Passengers waiting to board a tube train on the <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">London Underground</a> in the early 1900s (sketch by unknown artist)</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">London Underground</a>, the world's oldest underground railway, opened in 1863, and it began operating electric services using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fourth_rail" title="Fourth rail">fourth rail</a> system in 1890 on the <a href="/wiki/City_and_South_London_Railway" title="City and South London Railway">City and South London Railway</a>, now part of the <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">London Underground</a> <a href="/wiki/Northern_line" title="Northern line">Northern line</a>. This was the first major railway to use <a href="/wiki/Railway_electrification_in_Great_Britain" title="Railway electrification in Great Britain">electric traction</a>. The world's first deep-level electric railway, it runs from the <a href="/wiki/City_of_London" title="City of London">City of London</a>, under the <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">River Thames</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Stockwell" title="Stockwell">Stockwell</a> in south London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Drehstromtram_Lugano.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1502" data-file-width="1458" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Drehstromtram_Lugano.jpg/220px-Drehstromtram_Lugano.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Drehstromtram_Lugano.jpg/330px-Drehstromtram_Lugano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Drehstromtram_Lugano.jpg/440px-Drehstromtram_Lugano.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Drehstromtram_Lugano.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Maschinenfabrik_Oerlikon" title="Maschinenfabrik Oerlikon">Maschinenfabrik Oerlikon</a>'s first commercially AC-driven locomotive, the tramway in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lugano,_Switzerland" title="Lugano, Switzerland">Lugano, Switzerland</a>, 1896</div></div></div>
<p>The first practical <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">AC</a> electric locomotive was designed by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Eugene_Lancelot_Brown" title="Charles Eugene Lancelot Brown">Charles Brown</a>, then working for <a href="/wiki/Maschinenfabrik_Oerlikon" title="Maschinenfabrik Oerlikon">Oerlikon</a>, Zürich. In 1891, Brown had demonstrated long-distance power transmission, using <a href="/wiki/Three-phase_electric_power" title="Three-phase electric power">three-phase AC</a>, between a <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydro-electric plant</a> at <a href="/wiki/Lauffen_am_Neckar" title="Lauffen am Neckar">Lauffen am Neckar</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankfurt_am_Main" title="Frankfurt am Main">Frankfurt am Main</a> West, a distance of 280 km. Using experience he had gained while working for <a href="/wiki/Heilmann_locomotive" title="Heilmann locomotive">Jean Heilmann</a> on steam-electric locomotive designs, Brown observed that <a href="/wiki/AC_motor#Three-phase_AC_synchronous_motors" title="AC motor">three-phase motors</a> had a higher power-to-weight ratio than <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">DC</a> motors and, because of the absence of a <a href="/wiki/Commutator_(electric)" title="Commutator (electric)">commutator</a>, were simpler to manufacture and maintain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[b]</a></sup> However, they were much larger than the DC motors of the time and could not be mounted in underfloor <a href="/wiki/Bogie" title="Bogie">bogies</a>: they could only be carried within locomotive bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuffy2003129_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuffy2003129-43">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1894, Hungarian engineer <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1lm%C3%A1n_Kand%C3%B3" title="Kálmán Kandó">Kálmán Kandó</a> developed a new type 3-phase asynchronous electric drive motors and generators for electric locomotives. Kandó's early 1894 designs were first applied in a short three-phase AC tramway in <a href="/wiki/%C3%89vian-les-Bains" title="Évian-les-Bains">Évian-les-Bains</a> (France), which was constructed between 1896 and 1898.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1896, Oerlikon installed the first commercial example of the system on the <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Lugano" title="Trams in Lugano">Lugano Tramway</a>. Each 30-tonne locomotive had two 110 kW (150 hp) motors run by three-phase 750 V 40 Hz fed from double overhead lines. Three-phase motors run at constant speed and provide <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regenerative_brake" title="Regenerative brake">regenerative braking</a>, and are well suited to steeply graded routes, and the first main-line three-phase locomotives were supplied by Brown (by then in partnership with <a href="/wiki/Brown,_Boveri_%26_Cie" title="Brown, Boveri &amp; Cie">Walter Boveri</a>) in 1899 on the 40 km <a href="/wiki/List_of_railway_electrification_systems#Burgdorf-Thun_Bahn" title="List of railway electrification systems">Burgdorf–Thun line</a>, Switzerland.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ganz_engine_Valtellina.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="818" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Ganz_engine_Valtellina.jpg/220px-Ganz_engine_Valtellina.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Ganz_engine_Valtellina.jpg/330px-Ganz_engine_Valtellina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Ganz_engine_Valtellina.jpg/440px-Ganz_engine_Valtellina.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ganz_engine_Valtellina.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A prototype of a Ganz AC electric locomotive in Valtellina, Italy, 1901</div></div></div>
<p>Italian railways were the first in the world to introduce electric traction for the entire length of a main line rather than a short section. The 106 km <a href="/wiki/Valtellina" title="Valtellina">Valtellina</a> line was opened on 4 September 1902, designed by Kandó and a team from the Ganz works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuffy2003120–121_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuffy2003120–121-49">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patent_Office_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patent_Office-50">[48]</a></sup> The electrical system was three-phase at 3 kV 15 Hz. In 1918,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuffy2003137_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuffy2003137-51">[49]</a></sup> Kandó invented and developed the <a href="/wiki/Rotary_phase_converter" title="Rotary phase converter">rotary phase converter</a>, enabling electric locomotives to use three-phase motors whilst supplied via a single overhead wire, carrying the simple industrial frequency (50 Hz) single phase AC of the high voltage national networks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patent_Office_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Patent_Office-50">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>An important contribution to the wider adoption of AC traction came from SNCF of France after World War II. The company conducted trials at AC 50 Hz, and established it as a standard. Following SNCF's successful trials, 50 Hz, now also called industrial frequency was adopted as standard for main-lines across the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuffy2003273_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuffy2003273-52">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diesel_power_introduced">Diesel power introduced</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Diesel power introduced">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Diesel_locomotive" title="Diesel locomotive">Diesel locomotive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dieselisation#Rail_transport" title="Dieselisation">Dieselisation § Rail_transport</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Priestmann_Oil_Engine_-_Fig_150_p461_The_Steam_engine_and_gas_and_oil_engines_John_Perry.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1068" data-file-width="1894" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Priestmann_Oil_Engine_-_Fig_150_p461_The_Steam_engine_and_gas_and_oil_engines_John_Perry.PNG/220px-Priestmann_Oil_Engine_-_Fig_150_p461_The_Steam_engine_and_gas_and_oil_engines_John_Perry.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Priestmann_Oil_Engine_-_Fig_150_p461_The_Steam_engine_and_gas_and_oil_engines_John_Perry.PNG/330px-Priestmann_Oil_Engine_-_Fig_150_p461_The_Steam_engine_and_gas_and_oil_engines_John_Perry.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Priestmann_Oil_Engine_-_Fig_150_p461_The_Steam_engine_and_gas_and_oil_engines_John_Perry.PNG/440px-Priestmann_Oil_Engine_-_Fig_150_p461_The_Steam_engine_and_gas_and_oil_engines_John_Perry.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Priestmann_Oil_Engine_-_Fig_150_p461_The_Steam_engine_and_gas_and_oil_engines_John_Perry.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of Priestman Oil Engine from <i>The Steam engine and gas and oil engines</i> (1900) by John Perry</div></div></div>
<p>Earliest recorded examples of an internal combustion engine for railway use included a prototype designed by <a href="/wiki/William_Dent_Priestman" title="William Dent Priestman">William Dent Priestman</a>, which was examined by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_William_Thomson" title="Sir William Thomson">Sir William Thomson</a> in 1888 who described it as a <i>"[Priestman oil engine] mounted upon a truck which is worked on a temporary line of rails to show the adaptation of a petroleum engine for locomotive purposes."</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup> In 1894, a 20 hp (15 kW) two axle machine built by <a href="/wiki/Priestman_Brothers" title="Priestman Brothers">Priestman Brothers</a> was used on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hull_Docks" title="Hull Docks">Hull Docks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1906, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Diesel" title="Rudolf Diesel">Rudolf Diesel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Klose" title="Adolf Klose">Adolf Klose</a> and the steam and diesel engine manufacturer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gebr%C3%BCder_Sulzer" title="Gebrüder Sulzer">Gebrüder Sulzer</a> founded Diesel-Sulzer-Klose GmbH to manufacture diesel-powered locomotives. Sulzer had been manufacturing diesel engines since 1898. The Prussian State Railways ordered a diesel locomotive from the company in 1909. The world's first diesel-powered locomotive was operated in the summer of 1912 on the <a href="/wiki/Winterthur%E2%80%93Romanshorn_railway" title="Winterthur–Romanshorn railway">Winterthur–Romanshorn railway</a> in Switzerland, but was not a commercial success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup> The locomotive weight was 95 tonnes and the power was 883 kW with a maximum speed of 100 km/h.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup> Small numbers of prototype diesel locomotives were produced in a number of countries through the mid-1920s. The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> operated three experimental units of different designs since late 1925, though only one of them (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_locomotive_class_E_el-2" title="Russian locomotive class E el-2">E el-2</a>) proved technically viable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Limousin2010RVT01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Limousin2010RVT01.jpg/220px-Limousin2010RVT01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Limousin2010RVT01.jpg/330px-Limousin2010RVT01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Limousin2010RVT01.jpg/440px-Limousin2010RVT01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Limousin2010RVT01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Swiss</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">German</a> co-production: world's first functional diesel–electric railcar 1914</div></div></div>
<p>A significant breakthrough occurred in 1914, when <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Lemp" title="Hermann Lemp">Hermann Lemp</a>, a <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a> electrical engineer, developed and patented a reliable <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">direct current</a> electrical control system (subsequent improvements were also patented by Lemp).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> Lemp's design used a single lever to control both engine and generator in a coordinated fashion, and was the <a href="/wiki/Prototype" title="Prototype">prototype</a> for all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diesel%E2%80%93electric_locomotive" title="Diesel–electric locomotive">diesel–electric locomotive</a> control systems. In 1914, world's first functional diesel–electric railcars were produced for the <i>Königlich-Sächsische Staatseisenbahnen</i> (<a href="/wiki/Royal_Saxon_State_Railways" title="Royal Saxon State Railways">Royal Saxon State Railways</a>) by <a href="/wiki/Waggonfabrik_Rastatt" title="Waggonfabrik Rastatt">Waggonfabrik Rastatt</a> with electric equipment from <a href="/wiki/Brown,_Boveri_%26_Cie" title="Brown, Boveri &amp; Cie">Brown, Boveri &amp; Cie</a> and diesel engines from <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Swiss</a> <a href="/wiki/Sulzer_(manufacturer)" title="Sulzer (manufacturer)">Sulzer AG</a>. They were classified as <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Saxon_DET_1-2&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Saxon DET 1-2 (page does not exist)">DET 1 and DET 2</a> (<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/S%C3%A4chsischer_DET_1%E2%80%932" title="de:Sächsischer DET 1–2">de.wiki</a>). The first regular use of diesel-electric locomotives was in <a href="/wiki/Switcher" title="Switcher">switching</a> (shunter) applications. General Electric produced several small switching locomotives in the 1930s (the famous "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/44-tonner" title="44-tonner">44-tonner</a>" switcher was introduced in 1940) Westinghouse Electric and Baldwin collaborated to build switching locomotives starting in 1929.
</p><p>In 1929, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_National_Railways" title="Canadian National Railways">Canadian National Railways</a> became the first North American railway to use diesels in mainline service with two units, 9000 and 9001, from Westinghouse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High-speed_rail">High-speed rail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: High-speed rail">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/High-speed_rail" title="High-speed rail">High-speed rail</a></div>
<p>Although steam and diesel services reaching speeds up to 200 km/h were started before the 1960s in Europe, they were not very successful<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shinkansen_type_0_Hikari_19890506a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Shinkansen_type_0_Hikari_19890506a.jpg/220px-Shinkansen_type_0_Hikari_19890506a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Shinkansen_type_0_Hikari_19890506a.jpg/330px-Shinkansen_type_0_Hikari_19890506a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Shinkansen_type_0_Hikari_19890506a.jpg/440px-Shinkansen_type_0_Hikari_19890506a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shinkansen_type_0_Hikari_19890506a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>0-Series <a href="/wiki/Shinkansen" title="Shinkansen">Shinkansen</a>, introduced in 1964, triggered the intercity train travel boom.</div></div></div>
<p>The first electrified <a href="/wiki/High-speed_rail" title="High-speed rail">high-speed rail</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T%C5%8Dkaid%C5%8D_Shinkansen" title="Tōkaidō Shinkansen">Tōkaidō Shinkansen</a> was introduced in 1964 between <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osaka" title="Osaka">Osaka</a> in Japan. Since then <a href="/wiki/High-speed_rail" title="High-speed rail">high-speed rail</a> transport, functioning at speeds up to and above 300 km/h, has been built in Japan, Spain, France, Germany, Italy, the People's Republic of China, Taiwan (Republic of China), the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>. The construction of many of these lines has resulted in the dramatic decline of short-haul flights and automotive traffic between connected cities, such as the London–Paris–Brussels corridor, Madrid–Barcelona, Milan–Rome–Naples, as well as many other major lines.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="If this is correct, it's a strong argument in favor of building more high-speed rail lines. It would help to have quantitative estimates of the magnitude of this effect including as a function of distance with credible sources for those estimates. For example, could similar effects be obtained from high-speed rail between San Francisco and Los Angeles, where the distances are 60 percent more than between London, Paris and Brussels? (December 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>High-speed trains normally operate on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_gauge" title="Standard gauge">standard gauge</a> tracks of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continuously_welded_rail" title="Continuously welded rail">continuously welded rail</a> on <a href="/wiki/Grade_separation" title="Grade separation">grade-separated</a> <a href="/wiki/Right-of-way_(transportation)" title="Right-of-way (transportation)">right-of-way</a> that incorporates a large <a href="/wiki/Minimum_railway_curve_radius" title="Minimum railway curve radius">turning radius</a> in its design. While high-speed rail is most often designed for passenger travel, some high-speed systems also offer freight service.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preservation">Preservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Preservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heritage_railways" title="Heritage railways">Heritage railways</a></div>
<p>Since 1980, rail transport has changed dramatically, but a number of <a href="/wiki/Heritage_railway" title="Heritage railway">heritage railways</a> continue to operate as part of <a href="/wiki/Living_history" title="Living history">living history</a> to preserve and maintain old railway lines for services of tourist trains.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trains">Trains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Trains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
